                                                                                           A BSTRACT
008
009
                                                    We study methods for efficiently aligning large language models (LLMs) with
010                                                 human preferences given budgeted online feedback. We first formulate the LLM
011                                                 alignment problem in the frame of contextual dueling bandits. This formulation,
012                                                 subsuming recent paradigms such as online RLHF and online DPO, inherently
013                                                 quests for sample-efficient algorithms that incorporate online active exploration.
014                                                 Leveraging insights from bandit theory, we introduce a unified algorithm based on
015                                                 Thompson sampling and highlight its applications in two distinct LLM alignment
016                                                 scenarios. The practical agent that efficiently implements this algorithm, named
017                                                 SEA (Sample-Efficient Alignment), is empirically validated through extensive ex-
018
                                                    periments across three model scales (1B, 2.8B, 6.9B) and three preference learning
                                                    algorithms (DPO, IPO, SLiC). The results demonstrate that SEA achieves highly
019
                                                    sample-efficient alignment with oracle’s preferences, outperforming recent active
020
                                                    exploration methods for LLMs. We will release our codebase to hopefully accel-
021                                                 erate future research in this field.

038   win rates. SEA achieves the best sample efficiency for online alignment compared to XPO and APL.
039   1                                I NTRODUCTION
040
041   Aligning LLMs with human preferences is a crucial step to elicit various desirable behaviors, e.g.,
042   helpfulness and harmlessness (Bai et al., 2022). Moreover, it holds the potential to create superhu-
043
      man capabilities with only human-level feedback, as verifying is believed to be easier than synthesiz-
      ing novel behaviors. By iteratively generating massive new candidates and asking for human feed-
044
      back, LLMs could learn to reinforce good behaviors and may eventually surpass human capabilities.
045
046   Existing methods, either via reinforcement learning from human feedback (RLHF) (Stiennon et al.,
047   2020; Ouyang et al., 2022) or direct alignment from preferences (DAP) (Rafailov et al., 2023; Azar
048   et al., 2024), typically require a large amount of human annotations to achieve effective alignment.
049
      As a result, the volume of human feedback becomes a major bottleneck in practical alignment sce-
      narios. This poses a challenging and under-explored research question:
050
051                                                                            How to align LLMs sample-efficiently?
052
053   To seek answers, in Section 2, we formalize LLM alignment as a contextual dueling bandit
      (CDB) (Yue et al., 2012; Dudík et al., 2015), where the agent (i.e., the learner and decision maker, in


                                                                                                 1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      our case the LLM) interacts with the environment (i.e., human) to collect experience for improving
055   its policy. This formulation naturally calls for two key properties for alignment algorithms to be
056   sample-efficient:
057
      Property 1 (Online interaction). Interacting and learning online allows the agent to act with the
058   latest learned policy and then use that experience to immediately improve the policy.
059
      Property 2 (Active exploration). An actively exploring agent strategically selects actions such that
060
      the collected experience leads to maximal policy improvement.
061                                                                                                                       genera1:polished
062   Since the CDB formulation is general and almost subsumes all existing LLM alignment methods,                        these
063   it provides us a lens to scrutinize prior methods on the axes of Properties 1 and 2. In Section 3, we               two
      thoroughly discuss prior alignment approaches, ranging from offline learning (Rafailov et al., 2023;                para-
064
      Azar et al., 2024) and passive learning with iterative (Christiano et al., 2017; Dong et al., 2024) or on-          graphs
065
066   line interaction (Guo et al., 2024), to active exploration for learning preference models (Dwaracherla
067
      et al., 2024) or aligning LLMs (Muldrew et al., 2024; Zhang et al., 2024a; Xie et al., 2024). As will
      be revealed, most prior methods (partially) fail to satisfy the two properties, resulting in inferior sam-
068
      ple efficiency. Moreover, through the CDB formulation, we identify two LLM alignment scenarios,
069
      namely aligning from online users’ feedback (e.g., ChatGPT (2024)) and aligning from crowdsourc-
070   ing (Christiano et al., 2017; Ouyang et al., 2022), and shed light on their correspondences to two
071   bandit settings (explore & exploit and best arm identification). Understanding their differences is
072   important for designing efficient alignment algorithms for respective scenarios. We detail these two
073   settings in Section 2 and discuss how prior works approach them in Section 3.
074
      Leveraging algorithmic insights from bandit theory, our answer to the research question above is a
075
      principled alignment algorithm based on Thompson sampling (TS) (Thompson, 1933). Our method
076   fulfills Properties 1 and 2 to enhance sample efficiency, and it solves either of the two settings de-
077   pending on practical scenarios (Section 4.1). We incorporate techniques including epistemic reward
078   model, policy-guided search and mixed preference learning to implement the proposed TS algo-
079   rithm (Section 4.2), yielding a practical agent which we call SEA (Sample-Efficient Alignment).
080   In addition, we develop and open source a highly efficient, distributed learning system for studying
081   online LLM alignment methods (Section 5), eliminating barriers to fair empirical comparisons of
082   different alignment algorithms. Through extensive experiments (Section 6), SEA shows strong em-
083   pirical results (see Figure 1), consistently achieving higher win rates and improved sample efficiency
084
      compared to baseline approaches across three model scales. We will open source the codebase to
      hopefully accelerate future research in this field.
085
086   2       LLM ALIGNMENT AS CONTEXTUAL DUELING BANDITS
087
088   We first review the definitions and two typical objectives of Contextual Dueling Bandits (Sec-
089   tion 2.1), then translate them into the language of LLM alignment (Section 2.2). The tight connection
090   between them, as we will see, allows us to leverage insights from bandit algorithms to design effi-
091
      cient alignment algorithms for LLMs.
092   2.1       C ONTEXTUAL DUELING BANDITS
093
094   Contextual dueling bandits (CDB) (Yue et al., 2012; Dudík et al., 2015) is proposed to study online
095
      learning problems where the feedback consists of relative pairwise comparisons. A CDB problem
      can be characterized by a tuple (C, A, P), where C is the context space, A is the action space, and P :
096
      A×A×C 7→ [0, 1] denotes the unknown preference oracle. An agent learns by iteratively interacting
097
      with the environment (i.e., the preference oracle P) as follows. At each round t of the learning
098   process, a context ct ∼ pC is presented to the agent, who needs to take two actions at , a′t ∈ A for
099   a “dueling” comparison. The agent then receives stochastic feedback in the form of a comparison
100   result zt ∼ Ber (P (at ≻ a′t |ct )) from the environment, where Ber(·) is the Bernoulli distribution                fdaL
101   and ≻ denotes that the first action is preferred.
102
      Regret. The quality of the dueling actions selected by the agent is measured by the immediate
103
      regret: Rt = P(a⋆t ≻ at |ct ) + P(a⋆t ≻ a′t |ct ) − 1, where a⋆t is the best action1 the agent would
104   take at round t if it had complete knowledge of P. Intuitively, if the agent has learned how to act
105   optimally from round t onwards, it would no longer suffer any regret since its actions would be
106   indistinguishable from the best action (P(a⋆τ ≻ aτ |cτ ) = 21 hence Rτ = 0 for τ ≥ t).
107
          1
              We assume that a best action a⋆ in the sense that P(a⋆ ≻ a|c) ≥ 12 , ∀a ∈ A exists for all context c ∈ C.

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   Direct Alignment from Preferences                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        Contextual Dueling Bandit
122
123   Figure 2: (Left) A typical offline RLHF pipeline consists of three stages: collecting preference dataset, training
124   a reward model to serve as a proxy “environment”, and running RL to optimize the LLM policy inside the
125   proxy “environment”. (Middle) DAP simplifies RLHF by merging the last two stages of RLHF into a single
126   step of contrastive supervised learning. (Right) Unlike prior two offline approaches, online alignment employs
      a CDB interface, where the agent directly interacts with the preference oracle (e.g., humans) online, learning to
127
      produce good responses satisfying either E&E or BAI objectives. [genera1,fdaL: use fig.2 to provide a clearer
128   comparison between our CDB formulation and RLHF/DPO.]
129
130
      Optimal policy. A policy π ∈ ∆CA 2 associates each context c ∈ C with a probability distribution
      π(·|c) ∈ ∆A over the action space. The total preference of policy π over policy µ given a context
131
      sampling distribution pC ∈ ∆C and a preference oracle P is defined as
132
133
                          PpC ,P (π ≻ µ) = Ec∼pC Ea∼π(·|c) Ea′ ∼µ(·|c) [P(a ≻ a′ |c)] .               (1)
134   We adopt the von Neumann winner (Dudík et al., 2015) as the solution concept, which requires the
135   optimal policy π ⋆ to satisfy that
136                                                                        1
                                         ∀π ′ ∈ ∆CA , PpC ,P (π ⋆ ≻ π ′ ) ≥ .                         (2)
137                                                                        2
138   In words, the von Neumann winner policy should beat or tie with every policy (i.e., is zero-regret)
139   on average.
140   Learning objectives. The goal of bandit agents is to learn an optimal policy through interactions
141   with the environment. There are two subtypes of objectives that focus on different learning sce-
142   narios. The first type considers the conventional explore and exploit (E&E) setting (Robbins, 1952;
143   Auer et al., 2002), where the agent learns fully online and tries to minimize the cumulative regret
                       PT
144   over T rounds: t=1 Rt . The second type of objective concerns the best arm identification (BAI)
145   setting (Bubeck et al., 2009; Audibert & Bubeck, 2010), where the agent is only evaluated offline on
146   its average performance, possibly at any round (a.k.a., anytime regret), and tries to learn the optimal
147   policy with minimum interaction. Both settings call for effective online exploration strategies that
148
      satisfy Properties 1 and 2. Their differences will be made clearer with real scenarios in Section 2.2.
149   2.2   O NLINE ALIGNMENT AS CDB
150
151   Online LLM alignment can be framed as a CDB problem as illustrated in Figure 2 (Right). Specifi-
152
      cally, at time t a text prompt (cf. context) xt ∈ X is sampled from a prompt distribution pX . Then,
      two distinct responses (cf. actions), yt , yt′ ∈ Y, are chosen by the agent, and presented to human
153
      annotators (cf. the environment) for preference ranking. The winning and losing responses are la-
154
      beled as (yt+ , yt− ) based on a binary stochastic feedback zt ∼ Ber (P (yt ≻ yt′ |xt )). The agent                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 fdaL
155   is expected to produce good responses satisfying either E&E or BAI objectives, with knowledge
156   learned from the experience accumulated so far: Dt = {(xτ , yτ+ , yτ− )}tτ =1 . A standard assumption
157   is that human preferences follow the Bradley-Terry (BT) model (Bradley & Terry, 1952):
158                                         exp (r⋆ (xt , yt ))
159           P(yt ≻ yt′ |xt ) =                                           = σ(r⋆ (xt , yt ) − r⋆ (xt , yt′ )), (3)
                                 exp (r (xt , yt )) + exp (r⋆ (xt , yt′ ))

            We denote by ∆CA the set of all mappings C 7→ ∆A , where ∆A denotes the set of all probability distribu-
      tions over A.

      by substituting Eq. (3) into Eq. (1) and maximizing PpX ,P (π ≻ π ⋆ ) towards 1/2.
182   The two settings in bandits have their respective applications in LLM alignment. (1) The E&E
183   setting applies to the scenario of serving an LLM-based application online and aligning it continually
184   with users’ preferences. In this setting, the agent needs to balance exploration with exploitation,
185   thus the cumulative regret is of interest because the quality of every response matters. In fact,
186   commercial systems like ChatGPT would strategically ask users to make a dueling comparison,
187
      while upholding the quality of both responses. Please see Figure 12 in Appendix G for an example.
      (2) The BAI setting corresponds to the other scenario where annotators are paid to provide human
188
      feedback (Christiano et al., 2017; Ouyang et al., 2022). The desideratum in this scenario is to align
189
      the LLM at the minimum labeling cost, while the quality of the dueling responses is not important
190   as long as the experience helps sample-efficiently learn the von Neumann winner policy.
191
192   After formalizing LLM alignment in the framework of CDB and uncovering their tight connec-
193
      tions, we next thoroughly discuss existing alignment methods in the CDB framework and reveal the
      sources of their sample inefficiencies.

198   community. Previously, we used the term “agent” to denote the learner and decision maker, and
199   referred to its overall behavior as the “policy” π (as in Eq. (4)), following the standard abstraction in
200
      RL (Sutton & Barto, 2018; Sutton et al., 2022). However, in the LLM literature, “policy” typically
      refers to the generative language model alone, excluding components like reward models (RMs) that
201
      the agent might additionally build (see Figure 2 (Right)). To avoid confusion, from now on we use
202
      πθt to denote the generative language model (policy) and rϕt to denote the (optional) RM at time t,
203   both of which are learned from preference data Dt collected up to time t. We will omit t when the
204   time-indexing is not applicable (i.e., no online interaction) or not important in the context.

223
224
      which converts an online objective regarding the human’s implicit reward r⋆ to an offline objective
      regarding the proxy reward rϕ . The KL penalty in Eq. (7) is widely used for language model
225
      fine-tuning (Jaques et al., 2020; Xiong et al., 2024), and PPO (Schulman et al., 2017) has become a
226
      default RL optimizer to maximize the KL-regularized reward. However, the performance of RLHF
227   is guaranteed only if the preference data D induced by πref adequately covers π ⋆ (Zhu et al., 2023),
228   which is often approximated by updating πref with the latest (improved) πθ for re-sampling a batch
229   of online experience and repeating Eq. (5) and (7). Prior works typically focus on offline (Figure 2
230   (Left)) or iterative online (with only a few iterations) settings (Xiong et al., 2024; Dong et al.,
231   2024), which may compromise sample efficiency (Property 1).
232
      True online RLHF is difficult due to the complexity and instability of RL optimizers. For example,
233   Huang et al. (2024) openly reproduces offline RLHF scaling behaviors but requires many imple-
234   mentation tricks for training, highlighting the difficulties of an online counterpart. Fortunately, the
235   introduction of DAP (or direct optimizers) largely simplifies and stabilizes fine-tuning by conducting
236   contrastive supervised learning directly on D (Figure 3b). While most DAP works focus on learning
237   from a fixed offline preference dataset (Figure 2 (Middle), including Zhao et al. (2023); Rafailov
238   et al. (2023); Azar et al. (2024); Meng et al. (2024); Zhang et al. (2024b)), iterative DPO (Xu et al.,
239   2023) observes improved results when allowing iterative online interaction. Guo et al. (2024) fur-
240   ther propose OAIF to make DAP faithfully online, satisfying Property 1, and demonstrate that online
241
      learning prevents over-fitting and yields continual performance improvement. Nevertheless, it still
      employs passive exploration strategies (using y, y ′ ∼ πθ ), hindering sample efficiency (Property 2).
242
243   Online exploration in LLMs. A line of recent works (Mehta et al., 2023; Das et al., 2024; Melo
244   et al., 2024; Dwaracherla et al., 2024) adopts the fully online bandit formulation and incorporates ac-
245   tive exploration with uncertainty-aware RMs for response selection (Figure 3c). In particular, Mehta
246   et al. (2023) consider the E&E setting and develop a UCB-style (Auer et al., 2002) algorithm; Das
247
      et al. (2024) instead select the dueling responses with the most uncertain preference estimate, target-
      ing the BAI setting in a pure exploration way; unlike the above, Melo et al. (2024) view the problem
248
      from the angle of pool-based active learning and propose an acquisition function based on both en-
249
      tropy and epistemic uncertainty; finally, the work by Dwaracherla et al. (2024) is the closest to ours
250   in the sense that they apply double Thompson sampling (DTS) (Wu & Liu, 2016) for exploration,
251   but DTS is designed for the E&E setting while they evaluate anytime average performance as in
252   the BAI setting. We will show in Appendix E.1 that pure exploration by Das et al. (2024) is not
253   the best choice for BAI, and the objective mismatch in Dwaracherla et al. (2024) could lead to sub-
254   optimal performance in respective settings. Meanwhile, all these works primarily focus on learning
255   uncertainty-aware RMs online without updating LLM policies. Therefore, all responses are sampled
256   from a fixed proposal policy πβ (or even a fixed dataset), making the data coverage a critical concern.
257   Another line of research updates LLMs online while incorporating exploration. Zhang et al. (2024a)
258   and Xie et al. (2024) independently propose to learn an optimistic RM to encourage exploration.
259   They leverage the property of DPO (Rafailov et al., 2023) to reparameterize RM with policy and
260   conclude with an extra optimistic term in the DPO loss function. Thus, their learning processes are
261   like Figure 3b but with an optimistic direct optimizer. Muldrew et al. (2024) adopt the vanilla DPO
262   loss but utilize the implicit reward margin to actively select dueling responses. Yet, these methods are
263   tightly coupled with DPO and not compatible to other direct optimizers. Their experiments are also
264
      limited to a few online iterations, possibly due to the implementation difficulty of a faithfully online
      learning system. Given their relevance to our approach, we will reproduce them in a fully online
265
      manner for fair comparisons in Section 6.1. We summarize prior works in Table 3 in Appendix G.
266
267   4   SEA: SAMPLE - EFFICIENT ALIGNMENT FOR LLM S
268
269   In this section we present our online exploration agent SEA (Figure 3d). We first introduce a princi-
      pled Thompson sampling algorithm inspired by bandit theory (Section 4.1), and then derive SEA as

      Algorithm 1 Thompson sampling for LLM alignment (intractable).
271
         Input: Prompt distribution pX , unknown but queryable preference oracle P.
272
      1: Initialize experience D0 ← ∅.
273   2: for t = 1, . . . , T do
274   3:     Receive a prompt xt ∼ pX .
275   4:     Sample r ∼ pr (·|Dt−1 ) and set yt ← arg maxb∈Y r(xt , b).                    // Select 1st response y.
276         // E&E objective: aligning an online system.
      5:       repeat
277
                  Sample r ∼ pr (·|Dt−1 ) and set yt′ ← arg maxb∈Y r(xt , b).             // Select 2nd response y ′ .
278            until yt′ ̸= yt
279         // BAI objective: labeling via crowdsourcing.
280   6:    Set yt′ ← arg maxb∈Y V [σ (r(xt , yt ) − r(xt , b))],                   // OR select 2nd response y ′ .
281            where V [·] computes variance over the posterior pr (·|Dt−1 ).
            Query P to label {yt , yt′ }, and update experience Dt ← Dt−1 {(xt , yt+ , yt− )}.
                                                                           S
282
      7:
      8: end for
283                                                                       // See Algorithm 2 for a practical version.
284
285
286
287   its practically efficient implementation (Section 4.2). Interestingly, SEA can also be viewed as an
288   instantiation of a classical model-based RL architecture called Dyna (Sutton, 1990), for which we
289   defer the discussion to Appendix B.
290
      4.1     T HOMPSON SAMPLING FOR LLM ALIGNMENT
291
292   Thompson sampling (TS) (Thompson, 1933) is widely adopted for solving bandit problems at scale
293   due to its efficiency and strong empirical performance in general online learning problems (Chapelle
294   & Li, 2011; Russo et al., 2018). A bandit agent using Thompson sampling typically maintains and
295   incrementally updates a posterior distribution of the oracle reward p(r|D). Meanwhile, the agent
296   takes actions following a greedy policy with respect to a sampled RM: at = arg maxa r(a) with
297
      r ∼ pr (·|D). This simple yet effective algorithm naturally balances exploration and exploitation:
      when the agent has limited knowledge about the environment, the posterior estimate exhibits high
298
      uncertainty so that the sampled RM could guide the greedy policy to explore; after sufficient ex-
299
      perience is gathered, the sampled RM approximates the oracle more closely, allowing the agent to
300   exploit near-optimal policies.
301
302   In the context of LLM alignment, we leverage the BT assumption (Eq. (3)) to replace the preference
303
      oracle P with human’s implicit reward r⋆ . This substitution enables us to model the reward posterior
      p(r|D) in the standard TS framework, preserving the probabilistic structure necessary for effective
304
      posterior sampling. Inspired by prior works (Wu & Liu, 2016; González et al., 2017) on non-
305
      contextual K-arm bandits and preferential Bayesian optimization problems, we generalize them for
306   LLM alignment and develop a unified algorithm as shown in Algorithm 1. Note that we assume for
307   now the LLM agent can be fully described by the posterior p(r|D), and we defer practical reward
308   (rϕ ) and policy (πθ ) learning to Section 4.2.
309
      As Algorithm 1 presents, the first response of the duel is always selected via standard TS (Line 4).
310
      The selection of the second response varies across different settings. Line 5 will be used for scenarios
311
      where preference feedback is collected from online users (the E&E setting). The dueling responses
312   selected in this case will both try to maximize a sampled RM, so that the online user experience
313   is warranted with best effort. However, such algorithm can have poor asymptotic performance for
314   BAI problems (Russo, 2016), because sub-optimal responses with confidently high rewards might
315   be tried for a long time at the expense of not exploring other potentially better choices. In light
316   of this, Line 6 provides an alternative for scenarios where we could hire annotators for feedback
317   and low-quality but exploratory responses are safe to try. Specifically, Line 6 selects the second
318   response as the one that maximizes the variance of the preference (Eq. (3)) over the first response
319   yt . This variance quantifies the epistemic uncertainty of the RM, pointing the agent to the maximally
320
      informative direction to explore for better sample efficiency.
321   However, Algorithm 1 is yet to be practical for LLM alignment for three main reasons. First, com-
322   puting and sampling from a reward posterior is intractable for nearly all RMs at LLM scale, which
323   are mostly based on large transformers (Lambert et al., 2024). Second, even if we managed to ap-
      proximate the reward posterior, the arg max operations for response selection are still intractable


                                                            6
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


324
      since the search space Y is discrete and massive for token sequences of arbitrary length. Last but
325   not least, an LLM agent (Achiam et al., 2023; Touvron et al., 2023) typically consists in a generative
326   model πθ (e.g., a transformer (Vaswani et al., 2017)), while the algorithm above is centered around
327   a reward posterior p(r|D) that cannot be easily converted into a generative model. We next detail
328   how SEA practically addresses the three aforementioned issues.
329
330
      4.2     P RACTICAL IMPLEMENTATION
331   4.2.1    E PISTEMIC REWARD MODEL FOR POSTERIOR SAMPLING
332   To implement active exploration with TS, we seek an efficient way to maintain and incrementally
333   update the reward posterior p(r|D). We consider deep ensemble for our purpose, due to its capability
334   to model epistemic uncertainty (Lakshminarayanan et al., 2017) and provable results when applied to
335   TS in linear bandits (Qin et al., 2022). Specifically, we update a set of plausible RMs independently
336   and online, using the preference data and a regularized negative log-likelihood loss:                         RtS4
337                                              K
                                                 X
338                             LR (Φt |Dt ) =         Lr (ϕtk |Dt ) − λ||ϕtk − ϕ0k || ,                     (8)
339                                              k=1
340
      where Lr is defined in Eq. (5), Φt = {ϕtk }K k=1 contains the weights of the ensemble of size K,
341
      and λ controls the regularization towards individual initial weights ϕ0k . Each ensemble member               fdaL
342
      is initialized independently with random weights, and then trained with regularization to maintain
343   the diversity across ensemble members (Dwaracherla et al., 2024). Randomly picking a ϕtk from
344   Φt would approximate the posterior sampling (r ∼ pr (·|Dt )) for the RM (Lu & Van Roy, 2017;
345   Gustafsson et al., 2020). In practice, we train K MLP heads on top of a pretrained and frozen
346   transformer. We refer to the ensemble as the Epistemic Reward Model (ERM, denoted as RΦ ).
347
348   4.2.2    P OLICY- GUIDED SEARCH TO APPROXIMATE arg max
349   With the ERM approximating the reward posterior, we need to further approximate the response se-
350   lection steps (Lines 4 to 6) which generally take the form of arg maxb∈Y U (b), where U absorbs the
351   sampled prompt, the sampled RM, and optionally the selected first response (for BAI, Line 6). To
352   obtain the maximum, bandit algorithms for large action spaces typically resort to an action optimiza-
353   tion oracle (Katz-Samuels et al., 2020; Zhu et al., 2022), but they assume a linear structure of U with
354   respect to b, which might be impractical for LLMs. Therefore, we instead replace the optimization
355
      over Y with sampling from a policy-guided distribution conditioned on U , πprior (·|x) exp (U (·)/η),
      which is appropriate since it favors responses y that approximately maximize U (y). In practice, for
356
      a given prompt xt , we sample M candidate responses from the prior policy πprior (·|xt ) to construct
357
      a proposal set St = {yti }M i=1 . We then conduct a greedy search in St (taking η → 0) to identify the
358
      response yt (or yt′ ) that locally maximizes the utility function U , which is subsequently used in the
359
      duel. We also reuse the same St for different U functions at time t to save computation. The choice
360   of πprior will be discussed in the next section.
361
362   4.2.3    O NLINE POLICY LEARNING FROM MIXED PREFERENCES
363
      We finally resolve two remaining questions: (Q1) how to choose a sensible πprior at each time t
364   and (Q2) how to get a good generative policy online. To this end, we propose a simple approach to
365   approximately address both questions simultaneously. That is, we can utilize any direct optimizer to
366   learn the policy πθt online with the following loss and use the latest online policy as πprior :
367
368
                         Lπ (θt |Bt , πref , F ) = E(x,y+ ,y− )∼pBt Fθt (x, y + , y − , πref ) ,       (9)
369   where Bt is a batch of preference data labeled by the oracle wherein the responses are proposed by
370   πprior and selected by RΦt , F could be any DAP loss (see Appendix A for some examples), and πref
371   is chosen to be πsft . Note that we use πθt as πprior at any time t, thus B t is a batch of on-policy data.
372   By contrastive training on these on-policy data, we leverage their orthogonal benefits to achieve
373   maximal policy improvement (Tajwar et al., 2024; Tang et al., 2024).
374   Now that optimizing Eq. (9) yields a good online policy πθt (answering Q2), we need to assess
375   whether πθt can serve as a suitable πprior for approximating the arg max in TS (Q1). If we optimize
376   πθt with oracle preference data, St will be biased towards responses with high oracle reward r⋆ .
377   Bias towards high-r⋆ region is generally helpful because it aligns with arg maxb∈Y r(x, b) that
      seeks high-reward responses. However, optimizing πθt only with oracle data can average out the


                                                           7
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


378
      epistemic uncertainty of R, hindering the exploration efficiency. To mitigate this issue, we further
379   align πθt with RΦt using the same direct optimizer to encourage πθt to propose high-rϕtk responses
380
      for individual rϕtk , leading to better approximation of arg maxb∈Y r(x, b) for any sampled r. To
381
      implement, we optimize Eq. (9) over a mixture distribution pBtmix = γpBt + (1 − γ)pBtERM , where
382
383
      γ ∈ [0, 1] controls the mixture ratio and BtERM = {(xi , ỹi+ , ỹi− )}bi=1 consists of preference data
      labeled by randomly sampled individual ensemble members rϕtk . Interestingly, learning from mixed
384
      preferences further boosts sample efficiency because it utilizes the internal ERM to get pseudo labels
385
      instead of querying humans. This relates closely to model-based RL, for which we discuss further
386
      in Appendix B. We summarize our practical algorithm (Algorithm 2) in Appendix A.
387
388   5       E XPERIMENTAL SETUP
389   In this section, we elaborate the experimental setup employed to validate our algorithm and ensure
390   fair comparisons with other online alignment baselines. We start by introducing the distributed
391   learning system designed for experimenting with online LLM alignment using simulated human
392   preferences (Section 5.1). Then, we provide key experimental details in Section 5.2, with a full
393   description available in Appendix D.
394
      5.1      D ISTRIBUTED LEARNING SYSTEM
395
396   The interactive nature of LLM alignment necessitates an integrated online learning system that simu-
397   lates the interface depicted on the right of Figure 2. The absence of a performant open-source online
398   alignment system has restricted many existing works to only a few iterations of batch learning (Mul-
399   drew et al., 2024; Dong et al., 2024; Chen et al., 2024; Zhang et al., 2024a; Xie et al., 2024), which
400   creates a mismatch with their theories that typically require a large number of online interaction
401
      rounds. Even worse, such absence also makes the comparison between different LLM exploration
      methods difficult, often restricting evaluations to the simplest iterative DAP baselines (Zhang et al.,
402
      2024a; Xie et al., 2024).
403
                                                                                                Experience
404   To fill this gap, we build a highly efficient learning system for exper-
405   imenting with online LLM alignment algorithms. We notice that the              Learner            Actors
      computational bottleneck lies in online response sampling (i.e., au-           Workers
406                                                                                                  rs     vLLM
                                                                                                   te
407
      toregressive generation) and preference labeling (e.g., human, large                     rame Query
                                                                               DeepSpeed     Pa
      RMs, or large LLMs), which mirrors the slow actor-environment
408
      interaction seen in RL systems. Inspired by distributed deep RL                Learner
                                                                                                        Oracle
409                                                                                                      RM
      systems which spawn many actors or environments in parallel (Es-               Master                 Mosec
410   peholt et al., 2018; Weng et al., 2022), we design an Actor-Learner-                    Experience
411   Oracle architecture for online LLM alignment, which is depicted in
412   Figure 4. The three types of workloads (i.e., actor, learner and ora-
413   cle) are heterogeneous and require different optimization. In partic- Figure 4: The learning system
414   ular, we adopt vLLM (Kwon et al., 2023) for the actor to accelerate for experimenting online LLM
415   the autoregressive response generation. We also use DeepSpeed’s alignment algorithms.
416   ZeRO (Rasley et al., 2020; Rajbhandari et al., 2020) strategies to enhance the memory efficiency of
417   the learner. The updated model weights are broadcasted from the learner master to all actors after
418
      every optimizer step efficiently via NCCL, similar to Hu et al. (2024). Furthermore, to improve the
      scalability, we wrap the oracle RM as a service using Mosec (Yang et al., 2021b), which supports
419
      dynamic batching and parallel processing, to minimize preference query latency. Finally, we lever-
420
      age DeepMind Launchpad (Yang et al., 2021a) to compose all workloads into a distributed program
421   and adopt Plasma (Philipp & Robert, 2017) to efficiently transfer data across process boundaries.
422
423   We benchmark our system’s efficiency against a concurrent implementation of online DPO by Hug-
424
      gingFace4 , which utilizes only DeepSpeed for memory optimization. Our system achieves up to
      2.5× latency reduction compared to this counterpart, demonstrating its computational efficiency.
425
      Due to space constraints, detailed benchmarking methods and results are presented in Appendix F.

462   6        E MPIRICAL STUDIES
463
464
      We next present our empirical studies highlighting four results: (1) Comparisons with baselines
      across various direct optimizers and model scales demonstrate SEA’s superior sample efficiency
465
      (Section 6.1). (2) Ablations confirm that both online policy learning and active exploration con-
466
      tribute to sample-efficient alignment, and using the learned ERM for Best-of-N sampling further
467   improves the performance (Section 6.2). (3) Different exploration strategies (Line 5 or Line 6 in Al-
468   gorithm 1) are verified to work best in respective settings. (4) SEA robustly outperforms baselines
469   when GPT4o-mini is used as a judge to simulate human feedback. Results for (3-4) are deferred to
470   Appendices E.1 and E.2 due to space constraints.
471
472
      6.1         OVERALL COMPARISON
473   We first compare SEA with all baselines across three model scales and three direct optimizers.
474   APL and XPO are only compared when DPO is used as the direct optimizer, because they are
475   incompatible with IPO or SLiC. Figure 5 shows the win rate curves versus the number of query
476   steps. Across all settings, Online agents consistently improve sample efficiency over their Offline
477   counterparts, validating the necessity of Property 1 for alignment algorithms. Focusing on the first
478   row, we observe that among prior active exploration methods, XPO gives a small improvement in
479   final performance over Online (passive) at the 1B scale, but falls short for larger scales. On the
480
      other hand, APL shows a significant sample efficiency boost at the 1B scale, but this advantage
      diminishes when scaling up and it performs almost the same as Online at 6.9B scale. Our method,
481
      SEA, outperforms both offline and online passive methods across all scales and all direct optimizers,
482
      confirming the critical role that Property 2 plays for sample-efficient alignment. Meanwhile, in the
483   special case of using DPO as the direct optimizer, SEA also shows superior performance to prior
484   online active exploration methods including APL and XPO. We invite readers to revisit Figure 1,
485
          5
              https://huggingface.co/Skywork/Skywork-Reward-Llama-3.1-8B.

      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

495
      where we show that SEA not only attains significantly improved final performance (Left) but also
496
      achieves 2-5× better sample efficiency (Right).
497
498   Additionally, we note that the choice of direct optimizer matters for both online learning and
499   active exploration. When comparing different optimizers at 1B scale (the first column), all Offline
500
      agents demonstrate comparable learning efficiency and reach the same level of final performance
      (around 70% win rate), but SLiC Online agent deliver slightly less improvement than DPO and IPO
501
      Online agents. Besides, when incorporating active exploration, the SEA agent using DPO shows
502
      much larger improvement than the other two. This suggests that selecting the most suitable policy
503   optimizer coupled with active exploration would yield the best agent.
504

      cover two closely related baselines (Guo et al., 2024; Dwaracherla et al., 2024). We show in Fig-
      ure 6 the performance curves of each variant, all trained with DPO on 1B scale.

526
      7     C ONCLUSION
527
528   In this paper, we study the problem of LLM alignment through the lens of contextual dueling ban-
529   dits and propose a Thompson sampling-based algorithm to achieve sample-efficient alignment. We
530   incorporate three techniques, including epistemic reward model, policy-guided search and mixed
531
      preference learning to yield a practically efficient online alignment method. Extensive empirical
      evaluation demonstrates the superior sample efficiency of our method compared to existing base-
532
      lines. To our knowledge, this is the first work to study active exploration for online LLM alignment
533
      with fully online experimental verification. We hope our positive empirical results, along with the
534   open-sourced codebase, will encourage future research in this direction, ultimately enabling LLMs
535   to achieve superhuman intelligence with an affordable amount of human feedback.
536

      A      A LGORITHM DETAILS
811                                                                                                                              genera1:add-
812   While Algorithm 1 presents our Thompson sampling algorithm for LLM alignment, it is intractable and                        details
813   centered around the reward posterior modeling. We next present a practical sample-efficient alignment agent
814   that learns both an LLM policy and an epistemic reward model online.                                                       fdaL
815
816   Algorithm 2 Sample-efficient alignment (SEA) for LLMs
817        Input: Reference policy πref , DAP loss function F , prompt distribution pX , unknown but queryable
      preference oracle P, mixture ratio γ.
818
       1: Initialize experience D0 ← ∅, policy πθ0 ← πref , and ERM weights Φ0 = {ϕ0k }K k=1 randomly.
819
       2: for t = 1, . . . , T do
820    3:     Receive a prompt xt ∼ pX .
821    4:     Sample M responses yti ∼ πθt−1 (·|xt ) to construct St = {yti }M
                                                                             i=1 .
822    5:     Sample ϕ ∼ Uniform(Φt−1 ) and set yt ← arg maxb∈St rϕ (xt , b).            // Select 1st response y.
823         // E&E objective: aligning an online system.
       6:      repeat
824
                  Sample ϕ ∼ Uniform(Φt−1 ) and set yt′ ← arg maxb∈St rϕ (xt , b).                // Select 2nd response y ′ .
825
               until yt′ ̸= yt
826         // BAI objective: labeling via crowdsourcing.
827    7:      Set yt′ ← arg maxb∈St Vϕ [σ (rϕ (xt , yt ) − rϕ (xt , b))],                    // OR select 2nd response y ′ .
828                where Vϕ [·] computes variance across ensemble members of Φ .       t−1

829    8:      if g < γ for g ∼ Uniform(0, 1) then
                   Label {yt , yt′ } with P to obtain Bt = {(xt , yt+ , yt− )} and update experience Dt ← Dt−1 Bt .
                                                                                                                   S
830
               else
831                Use RΦt−1 to get synthetic labels and obtain Bt = {(xi , ỹi+ , ỹi− )}.
832            end if
833    9:      Update ERM with the regularized NLL loss (Eq. (8)):
834
                                                  Φt ← Φt−1 − αR ∇Φ LR (Φt−1 |Dt ).
835
836                                                                                                      // Reward learning.
      10:       Update policy with the direct optimizer (Eq. (9)):
837
838                                             θt ← θt−1 − απ ∇θ Lπ (θt−1 |Bt , πref , F ).
839
                                                                                                         // Policy learning.
840   11: end for
841
842   In Algorithm 2, we describe an online setting where a single example is processed at each time t (batch size
843   b = 1). This is mainly for notational convenience, while in implementation we set b to be the training batch
844   size (e.g., 128). We instantiate the reward posterior with an epistemic reward model, which allows for efficient
845
      incremental update and sampling. We also replace the global optimization (arg maxb∈Y ) with a policy-guided
      local search among proposals sampled from the latest online policy πθt−1 . At each time t, we update ERM
846   weights Φ with m gradient steps with randomly sampled batches from the experience Dt . We find setting
847   m = 5 suffices to achieve a reasonable accuracy. The policy parameters θ are updated using mixed preference
848   data, with a γ proportion being the real environment experience and the remaining (1 − γ) from the ERM’s
849   synthetic experience. Note that the synthetic experience is not added into Dt to ensure reward learning always
850
      uses ground truth environment data.
851   We consider the following three direct optimizers in our experiments:
852
               • DPO (Rafailov et al., 2023):

855                                                                              πref (y + |x) πθ (y − |x)
856            • IPO (Azar et al., 2024):
857                                                                                             !      !2
                                                                       πθ y + |x πref y − |x

                                            +     −                                                  1
858                               Fθ (x, y , y , πref ) =       log                               −                      (11)

864
      B    O N CONNECTIONS WITH SINGLE - STEP RL
865                                                                                                                      genera1
866   By viewing contextual dueling bandits as single-step preference-based RL (PbRL) (Busa-Fekete et al., 2014;
867   Wirth et al., 2017) problems, we can interpret paradigms shown in Figure 3 from the RL perspective.
868
      RLHF approaches (Figure 3a) are instances of offline model-based RL (Kidambi et al., 2020; Yu et al., 2021;        fdaL
869   Schrittwieser et al., 2021; Liu et al., 2023; Tajwar et al., 2024), where they learn a reward model (no need for
870   a transition model since the prompt-response interaction is single-step) of the environment from a batch of
871   offline collected data, and train a policy (i.e., LLM) to maximize the return (i.e., expected one-step reward)
872   with respect to the learned reward.
873   In contrast, DAP methods (Figure 3b) are similar to policy-based model-free RL algorithms, e.g., REIN-
874   FORCE (Williams, 1992) which conducts policy gradient update:
875                                    Ex∼X Ey∼πθ (·|x) [R(x, y)∇θ log πθ (y|x)] ,                               (13)
876
      where R(x, y) is the return (i.e., cumulative reward) of the trajectory. To connect with DAP, we could set R as
877
      arbitrary scalar values based on the binary preference outcomes, e.g., R(x, y + ) = ζ and R(x, y − ) = −ζ for
878   preference triplet {x, y + , y − }. In this way we could rewrite Eq. (13) as
879
                      Ex∼X Ey,y′ ∼πθ (·|x) E(y+ ≻y− )∼P ζ ∇θ log πθ (y + |x) − ∇θ log πθ (y − |x) ,

880                                                                                                              (14)
881   by repeating action sampling twice and querying the oracle for preference labeling. This matches the gradient
882   direction of contrastive DAP losses (e.g., see Section 4 of DPO (Rafailov et al., 2023)) if we optimize them
883   online (Guo et al., 2024).
884   Additionally, active reward learning from behavior policy’s data distribution (Figure 3c) can be regarded
885   as inverse RL (Ng & Russell, 2000), which tries to recover environment’s reward function given expert
886   trajectories. In the context of LLM alignment, the preference data {x, y + , y − }N
                                                                                        i=1 directly encodes human’s
      implicit reward r⋆ , which can be inversely learned with assumptions such as the BT model (Bradley & Terry,
887
      1952). However, existing methods belonging to this paradigm mostly rely on a fixed (and suboptimal) behavior
888   policy for response sampling, whose coverage inherently limits the quality of the recovered reward function.
889
      Last but not least, SEA depicted in Figure 3d resembles a class of online model-based RL algorithms, known
890
      as Dyna (Sutton, 1990; Janner et al., 2019), that learns a world model from environment experience and
891   trains a base agent (consisting of reactive policies and value functions) from both environment experience and
892   model experience. Compared to model-free methods, Dyna naturally enables more sample-efficient learning
893   by planning with the learned world model to update the base agent. In SEA, we learn the reward model
894   online and update the LLM (i.e., the reactive policy) with model-planing experience by mixed preference
      learning (Section 4.2.3). Online model-based RL algorithms could suffer from catastrophic forgetting in the
895
      face of nonstationary data (Liu et al., 2024b), and we leave it for future work. Overall, this model-based RL
896   formulation is powerful and explains popular LLM techniques, e.g., Best-of-N sampling (Touvron et al., 2023)
897   can be viewed as planning for acting, which trades compute for performance. We believe it is a promising path
898   leading us to unlock superhuman capabilities of LLMs.
899
900   C    BASELINE METHODS
901
                                                                                                                         fdaL
902   We review four baseline methods that are relevant to this work and used for comparisons in our experiments.
903   Offline DAP. We review DPO (Rafailov et al., 2023), which is a representative work in the direction of Direct
904   Alignment from Preferences (DAP). It simplifies the two-stage pipeline of offline RLHF (Figure 2 (Left)) as a
905   single step of supervised learning (Figure 2 (Middle)) by leveraging the closed-form solution (Peters & Schaal,
      2007; Peng et al., 2019) of the RL objective in Eq. (7):
906
907                                                  1                  1
                                       πr (y|x) =        πref (y|x) exp( r(x, y)),                               (15)
908                                                 Z(x)                β
909   where Z(x) normalizes such that Σy πr (y|x) = 1, to reparametrize r as a function of π:
910
                                                          πr (y|x)
911                                     r(x, y) = β log              + β log Z(x).                               (16)
                                                          πref (y|x)
912
913   Consequently, plugging Eq. (16) into the reward model loss (Eq. (5)) yields a contrastive loss that directly
914
      optimizes the policy:
                                              "                                          !#
915                                                           πθ y + |x πref y − |x

916                        min E(x,y+ ,y− )∼pD − log σ β log                                ,                 (17)
                            πθ                                 πref (y + |x) πθ (y − |x)
917
      where D is a pre-collected offline preference dataset.


                                                               17
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


918
      We also experiment different DAP methods6 besides DPO, such as IPO (Azar et al., 2024) and SLiC (Zhao
919   et al., 2023), whose loss functions are shown in Eq. (11) and (12).
920
      Online DAP (Guo et al., 2024). In contrast to the conventional DAP methods that learn a policy from a
921
      fixed dataset D, online DAP proposes to collect on-policy preference data to update the policy online. It first
922   samples responses from the current policy (y, y ′ ) ∼ πθt , then acquires preference labels to form a batch
923   Bt = {(x, y + , y − )}bi=1 . One gradient step minimizing the DAP loss over this data batch to get πθt+1 , which
924   is used for the next iteration. Such approach not only mitigates the over-fitting issue faced by offline DAP
925   methods (Guo et al., 2024), but also facilitates online interaction (Property 1) with the environment, falling into
926   the second paradigm of CDB solution algorithms (Figure 3b).
927   Active Preference Learning (APL) (Muldrew et al., 2024). APL follows the online DAP paradigm, but is
928   restricted to DPO due to its reliance on DPO implicit rewards. Two techniques are proposed by APL to actively
929   select both prompts and dueling responses for querying the preference oracle:
930
                1. Predictive entropy (PE) for selecting prompts. In this step APL computes a Monte-Carlo esti-
931                mate of PE for each prompt as Hπθ (y|x) ≈ −ΣN     n=1 log πθ (yn |x)/N , where yn ∼ πθ (·|x) and
932                log πθ (yn |x) is the summation of log probabilities of each token. Then, APL filters a subset of
933                prompts with high PE to form XS .
934             2. Preference model certainty for selecting dueling responses. For prompts in XS , APL generates many
935                responses for each prompt, then selects the pair with largest reward margin measured as |r̂(xi , yi ) −
936                r̂(xi , yi′ )|, where r̂ is the DPO implicit reward r̂(x, y) = β(log πθ (y|x) − log πref (y|x)).
937   By above two steps, APL actively explores more uncertain prompts and responses in an online DPO paradigm,
938   satisfying both Properties 1 and 2.
939
      Exploratory Preference Optimization (XPO) (Xie et al., 2024). XPO studies LLM alignment in the frame-
940   work of token-level MDP, and leverages the property that DPO conducts implicit Q⋆ -approximation (Rafailov
941   et al., 2024), so that
942                                        π ⋆ (y|x)
                                     β log            = r⋆ (x, y) − V ⋆ (x) ∀y,                             (18)
943                                        πref (y|x)
944   where V ⋆ is the optimal value function depending only on the prompt x. XPO incorporates the implicit (global)
945   optimism for exploration by overestimating the value Vπθ (x) = r⋆ (x, y) − β log ππrefθ (y|x)
                                                                                               (y|x)
                                                                                                     . This is achieved
946   by optimizing the policy with a modified DPO loss:
947                                        "                                                              !#
                                                                             πθ y + |x πref y − |x

                                                       ref
948            min E(x,y+ ,y− ,yref )∼p t α log πθ (y |x) − log σ β log                                      ,     (19)
949
                πθ                      B                                     πref (y + |x) πθ (y − |x)
950
      where y ref ∼ πref (·|x) and Bt is an on-policy data batch in the same vein as online DPO. Intuitively, the
951   first term in Eq. (19) biases the policy toward a large value estimation such that Vπθ ≳ V ⋆ , implementing
952   the optimism in the face of uncertainty (OFU) for exploration. Theoretically, Xie et al. (2024) also prove the
953   sample complexity bound of XPO, making it a promising algorithm for online LLM alignment.
954   Self-exploring language model (SELM) (Zhang et al., 2024a) is a concurrent work of Xie et al. (2024) that
955   proposes nearly the same theoretic algorithm to achieve OFU. However, the practical implementation of SELM             cF2f
956   involves offline preference dataset for training, making it hard to benchmark in an online alignment setting like
957   ours. Therefore, we will keep XPO as our baseline while omitting the comparison with SELM.
958
959   D        F ULL EXPERIMENTAL DETAILS
960
      In the main text we focus on the task of summarization using the TL;DR dataset. This provides a lightweight
961   and clean setting to extensively study different algorithmic designs with affordable computational resources.
962   Appendix D.1 provides the full details of this setting.
963
      To further validate the sample efficiency of SEA in aligning LLMs to perform general tasks, we adopt the
964   UltraFeedback dataset (Cui et al., 2023) and evaluate trained LLMs on AlpacaEval 2.0 (Li et al., 2023). Ap-
965   pendix D.2 provides more details of this setting.                                                                      z8J2
966
967   D.1       D ETAILS OF TL;DR TASK
968
      Models. We experiment three model scales (1B, 2.8B, 6.9B) from the Pythia family (Biderman et al., 2023).
969
      We take pretrained SFT models from Huang et al. (2024) as πref for the starting model in all experiments.
970   Except in Section 6.1, we use 1B model for other experiments to save computation.
971
          6
              We use “DAP method” and “direct optimizer” interchangeably.


                                                                18
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


972
       Preference oracle. We simulate the process of human feedback with a strong scalar RM and refer it as
973    preference oracle. We choose Skywork-Reward-Llama-3.1-8B7 (Liu et al., 2024a), which is top-ranked in
974    RewardBench leaderboard (Lambert et al., 2024), as the preference oracle.
975
       Epistemic reward model. We build ERM on top of a pretrained 0.4B transformer (Jiang et al., 2023), by re-
976    moving its head and adding an ensemble of MLPs. The size of ensemble is set to K = 20, and all MLPs contain
977    2 hidden layers of 128 nodes. Note that the ERM is chosen to be much smaller than the preference oracle fol-
978    lowing Dwaracherla et al. (2024), which reflects the fact that human preferences can be more complex than what
979    the agent can model. The regularization coefficient λ is fixed to be 0.5 after a coarse hyperparameter search.
980    Data. We employ the widely adopted TL;DR dataset (Stiennon et al., 2020) for our experiments. It consists of
981    Reddit posts as prompts, and the agent is required to give summaries that align with human preferences. We fix
982    50k prompts for training and limit the query budget to 50k as well.
983    DAP methods. We adopt three DAP methods (direct optimizers) to thoroughly validate our algorithm, includ-
984    ing DPO (Rafailov et al., 2023), IPO (Azar et al., 2024) and SLiC (Zhao et al., 2023). Except in Section 6.1,
985    all experiments are done with DPO as the direct optimizer.                                                             fdaL
986    Baselines. Similar to Guo et al. (2024), we include the offline and online variants of different DAP methods as
987    baselines. Additionally, we compare with two active exploration baselines built on online DPO: APL (Muldrew
988    et al., 2024) and XPO (Xie et al., 2024). A detailed review of all baselines can be found in Appendix C.
989    Metrics. We use the win rate of agent’s responses against reference responses judged by the preference oracle
990    as the performance metric. This metric can reflect both the agent’s cumulative regret and anytime regret (i.e., av-
991    erage performance). In the E&E setting, we measure the “online” win rate of the agent’s dueling responses that
       are executed during experience collection and take the average. In the BAI setting, we measure the “offline” win
992
       rate by evaluating the latest agent’s responses given a fixed set of 1000 holdout prompts periodically. We mainly
993    focus on the BAI setting because crowdsourcing seems a major scenario for most practitioners, and present one
994    set of experiments for comparing different exploration strategies in both settings. When the comparison is only
995    made within a model scale, we report the relative win rate against the initial STF models. When the comparison
996    is across scales (Figure 1 Left), we report the absolute win rate against the ground truth responses in the dataset.
997    Hyperparameters. We set β = 0.1 for DPO and β = 0.2 for SLiC and find they are robust for all scales.
998    We tune β from {0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 1.0} for IPO across scales and report the best performing results. We sample
999    M = 20 on-policy responses with a temperature η = 0.7 during training, and use greedy decoding for offline
       evaluation (BAI’s metric). We use the Adam optimizer with learning rate of 5 × 10−7 and cosine scheduling,
1000
       and set the batch size to be 128. We initialize the mixture ratio γ of SEA to be 1 and adjust it to 0.7 after a
1001   burn-in period of 1k samples.
1002   All hyperparameters are kept the same for offline and online baselines, except that online methods update the          fdaL
1003   sampling policy after every gradient step as the latest πθt . For APL and XPO, we keep the learning rate and
1004   DPO’s β the same for apple-to-apple comparisons. Specifically for APL, we initially sample 1024 prompts per
       batch and use the predictive entropy to filter a subset of 128 prompts. Then, we sample 8 responses per prompt
1005
       and use the preference model certainty to finalize two responses for the duel. Specifically for XPO, we follow
1006   the their recommended optimism coefficient to set α = 5 × 10−6 .
1007
       Statistical significance. There are various factors to introduce randomness during online learning. We thus
1008
       launch 3 independent runs for every experiment with different random seeds. All the results are reported with
1009   mean and standard error to indicate their statistical significance.
1010
       Computational resources. Experiments at all scales are conducted on a single machine with 8 A100 GPUs
1011
       to run the learner and actors. We additionally host a separate remote server with workers spawned on 16 A100
1012   GPUs for the oracle RM8 , so that it can be queried by all concurrently running experiments. All experiments
1013   conducted for this research consume about 2 A100 GPU years.

1039   Data. We take the UltraFeedback dataset (Cui et al., 2023), which is widely used for LLM alignment in the
1040   literature. We filter out samples whose prompt is longer than 1800 tokens and result in 61k samples. We extract
1041
       prompts from the filtered dataset while excluding the responses. The prompt set are collected from multiple
       sources and cover diverse domains, making it suitable to improve LLM’s capability on general tasks.
1042
1043   DAP method and baselines. We employ the state-of-the-art DAP method, SimPO (Meng et al., 2024), as our
1044
       direct optimizer. Since SimPO is originally an offline algorithm, we extend it to Online SimPO and take both
       offline and online variants as baselines.
1045
1046   Evaluation. We evaluate SEA and baselines using AlpacaEval 2.0 (Li et al., 2023). It consists of 805 test
1047
       prompts, and uses GPT4-Turbo to judge the quality of model responses against reference responses generated
       by GPT-4-Turbo. We follow the standard protocol to report both the win rate (WR) and the Length-Controlled
1048   win rate (LC) (Dubois et al., 2024).
1049
1050
       Hyperparameters. We follow SimPO’s recommended hyperparameters to set β = 10 and γ/β = 0.3. We
       use a learning rate of 8 × 10−7 and batch size of 128. The decoding temperature is set to be 0.9 for generating
1051   evaluation outputs. The same hyperparameters apply to baselines and our method. Configurations of SEA are
1052   kept the same as those in the TL;DR task (Appendix D.1).
1053
1054   E       E XTENDED EMPIRICAL STUDIES
1055
1056   We present additional empirical studies in this section, including investigation on different exploration strate-
1057   gies (Appendix E.1) and preference oracles (Appendix E.2) on the TL;DR task, as well as the performance
       comparison on AlpacaEval 2.0 for general tasks (Appendix E.3).
1058
1059
       E.1        C HOICE OF EXPLORATION STRATEGIES
1060
1061   Recalling that different LLM alignment scenarios (online system or crowdsourcing) require different explo-
1062   ration strategies to meet their respective learning objectives (Section 2.2). We investigate three strategies based
1063   on posterior sampling and compare them on both online and offline performance. The first strategy (Uncer-
       tainty) focuses on pure exploration with information maximization. It seeks the pair of dueling responses that
1064
       exhibits the largest epistemic uncertainty, which is implemented by selecting the pair whose logits difference
1065   has the largest variance across ensemble members. The second (E&E-TS) and the third (BAI-TS) strategies
1066   follow the principles in Algorithm 1, and their differences are between Line 5 and Line 6. The comparison
1067   results are shown in Figure 7 (Left and Middle). Focusing on the left plot, we observe that E&E-TS strategy
1068   achieves the best online performance, which is within our expectation. In contrast, Uncertainty shows the
       worst online performance because it tries to maximize the information gain but does not prioritize reward
1069
       maximization. On the other hand, conclusions are interestingly different when taking the offline performance as
1070   the metric. In this case, BAI-TS and Uncertainty both exhibit more efficient offline performance improvement
1071   than E&E-TS. This can be attributed to that exploration for uncertainty minimizing helps to identify more
1072   informative responses to train the LLM policy. Moreover, BAI-TS ≻ Uncertainty indicates exploration with
1073   both reward and information maximization is better than exploration with only information maximization.
       E&E-TS, however, always chooses two responses with similarly high quality to exploit. This can not only lead
1074
       to less efficient exploration, but also result in less efficient policy learning due to smaller DAP loss gradients.
1075
1076   E.2        A LIGNING LLM S WITH A HUMAN SIMULATOR
1077   Results presented so far are based on experimenting LLM alignment with the preference oracle being a scalar
1078   reward model, which is deterministic and does not capture the potential randomness of the choice by real
1079   humans. To test different agents in a more realistic setting, we use generative models as human simulator in an
       LLM-as-a-judge (Bubeck et al., 2023; Zheng et al., 2023) manner. In particular, we directly query the OpenAI

1093   API and use gpt-4o-mini-2024-07-18 as the judge to provide preference feedback. We use a similar prompt
1094   template to Li et al. (2023)’s, which is shown in Figure 11. We also randomly swap the order of two responses                   fdaL
1095   to mitigate the known position bias of LLM judges. The results are shown in Figure 7 (Right). We can observe
       the performance curves generally exhibit higher variance, possibly due to the randomness introduced in the
1096
       feedback process, which puts more stringent requirements for learning algorithms. The two active exploration
1097   methods demonstrate opposite results to those in Section 6.1—APL learns fast initially but is eventually out-
1098   performed by Online, while XPO improves over Online after stabilizing its training and delivers a better final
1099   performance. Our agent, SEA, is shown to offer the best sample efficiency as well as asymptotic performance,
1100   further validating the importance of online learning and well-designed active exploration mechanism.
1101
       E.3                      P ERFORMANCE ON GENERAL TASKS
1102                                                                                                                                   z8J2,RtS4,fdaL
1103   We investigate the generalizability of SEA by training with the prompt set from UltraFeedback (Cui et al.,
1104   2023) and evaluating the model performance on AlpacaEval 2.0 (Li et al., 2023). Figure 8 shows the Length-
1105   Controlled (LC) win rate of different models against GPT-4-Turbo. The left plot compares the sample efficiency
1106
       (in terms of the number of queries) of offline, online and SEA SimPO. The results suggest that enabling online
       interaction does not improve the sample efficiency over the offline counterpart. Such observation is in stark
1107   contrast to what we have seen in the TL;DR task, where the online agent always improves over the offline ones.
1108   We hypothesize that this is due to the different coverage of πref in these two tasks. For TL;DR, which is a
1109   much easier task, the initial SFT models already have good coverage, permitting online DAP with only passive
1110   exploration to work reasonably well; however, for more challenging tasks, the insufficient coverage of πref
1111
       would lead to sample complexity exponential in β1 (Xie et al., 2024), which necessitates deliberate exploration,
       such as Thompson sampling proposed in this work. The above claim is justified by observing that SEA largely
1112
       improves the sample efficiency over the online and offline variants.
1113
1114   Attentive readers may have noticed that comparing query budget could be advantageous to SEA because pseudo
       labels are used in mixed preference learning (Section 4.2.3), which results in more gradient steps given the
1115
       same query budget. In the right plot of Figure 8, we show the performance versus gradient step. We can
1116   observe SEA has the steepest learning curve, verifying that it explores more informative samples to yield faster
1117   improvement.
1118
       Last but not least, in Table 2, we show the AlpacaEval 2.0 LC win rates of XPO and SELM (as reported in
1119   their papers), along with ours and several cutting-edge LLMs. SEA is agnostic to direct optimizers, thus it can
1120   leverage the state-of-the-art SimPO to achieve a high LC of 47.4%. On the other hand, XPO and SELM can only
1121   be applied to DPO, restricting their potential to incorporate future advances in direct optimization algorithms.
