1149   and exclusively trained RMs13 . This is similar to the settings in our experiments (see Appendix D) except that
1150   we fix the preference oracle to be a strong general-purpose RM.
1151   Hardware & Software. All benchmarking experiments are conducted on a single machine with eight A100-
1152   40G GPUs and 96 AMD EPYC 7352 CPUs. To ensure fair comparison, we align all key hyperparameters
1153   for both our codebase and trl. The DeepSpeed ZeRO-2 strategy is employed by default when GPU memory
1154   suffices; otherwise, ZeRO-3 or ZeRO-2-offload is utilized as applicable. Notably, the distributed architec-
1155
       ture of our implementation provides flexibility in system configuration, enabling adjustments to accommodate
       memory and computational time constraints. Figure 9 illustrates two example configurations employed in our
1156   benchmarking experiments. We will provide all benchmarking scripts in our codebase for reproducibility.
1157
1158          • Config 1 collocates all three workloads on each of the GPUs. Specifically, eight vLLM instances
1159            (for actors) and eight Mosec workers (for oracle RMs) are spawned to run independently on each
1160            GPU. After a batch of responses is generated (by actors) and labeled (by oracle RMs), it is sent to
                the learner, which runs on all eight GPUs coordinated through ZeRO strategies for policy learning.
1161
                The updated policy weights are then broadcasted to all actors for on-policy response sampling on
1162            subsequent prompt batch. While this configuration maximizes GPU utilization, it requires substantial
1163            GPU memory to accommodate all workloads and is thus employed only for 1B scale experiments.
1164          • Config 2 only collocates actor and oracle workloads on half of the GPUs, reserving the remaining
1165            four GPUs exclusively for the learner. This is suited for larger-scale experiments (e.g., 2.8B or 6.9B),
1166            where additional GPU memory is allocated to the learner. However, this setup incurs idle time on
1167            half of the GPUs due to data dependency, as the learner must await new preference data, and the
                actor must await updated policies. An alternative is to implement asynchronous data collection,
1168
                where minor data staleness is allowed by using θt−1 to generate data for updating θt . Although this
1169            data would not be strictly on-policy, asynchronous training could reduce idle time and enhance GPU
1170            utilization. This approach has proven effective in large-scale RL systems (Berner et al., 2019), and
1171            we leave this optimization to future work.
1172
       Results. Benchmarking results for the latency of training a batch of 128 samples are presented in Figure 10.
1173
       Overall, training with the config 2 demonstrates consistently greater efficiency than trl, achieving up to a 2.5×
1174   reduction in latency at the 2.8B scale.
1175
       We next analyze the time costs for individual stages: generate, oracle and learn. Across all scales and configu-
1176
       rations, ours demonstrates significantly lower generate time than trl, due to distributed actors utilizing vLLM.
1177   Additionally, at the 6.9B scale, ours requires substantially less oracle time than trl, as trl employs ZeRO-3
1178   to prevent GPU memory overflow, thereby slowing inference. In contrast, ours config 2 allows for flexible col-
1179   location, enabling oracle RMs hosted via Mosec to operate in parallel without sharding. However, ours config
1180   2 incurs longer learn time compared to trl due to the use of only half the available GPUs. This limitation also
       explains why, at the 1B scale, config 2 has higher latency than config 1 across all stages.
1181
1182   The other category accounts for time costs associated with data loading, tokenization, and communication.
1183   Here, inter-process communication is the primary cost, with trl showing minimal overhead as all three stages
       operate within the same process on identical micro-batches, avoiding weight synchronization. By contrast,
1184
       ours requires considerable time to transfer updated policy weights from the learner to all actors. While NCCL

       is recommended for synchronization over GLOO, it requires older vLLM packages (prior to version 0.4.3),
1206   which may lack support for newer LLM architectures. Moreover, NCCL is incompatible with config 1 due to

1231   newly collected preference data, while πβ denotes a fixed proposal policy. Algorithms that encompass online
1232   interaction (Property 1), active exploration (Property 2), and learnable πθ offer the best sample efficiency.

1234   them for comparisons in our experiments.

1257       LLM-as-a-Judge Prompt Template
1258
          I require a leaderboard for various large language models. I’ll provide you with prompts
1259
          given to these models and their corresponding outputs. Your task is to assess these responses,
1260
          and select the model that produces the best output from a human perspective.
1261      ## Instruction
1262      {instruction}
1263      ## Model Outputs
1264      Here are the unordered outputs from the models. Each output is associated with a specific
1265      model, identified by a unique model identifier.
1266      {
1267      "model_identifier: "0",
1268      "output": {response0}
1269
          }
          {
1270
          "model_identifier: "1",
1271
          "output": {response1}
1272      }
1273      ## Task
1274      Evaluate the models on the basis of the quality and relevance of their results, and select
1275      the model that generated the best result. Reply with the identifier of the best model. Our
1276      evaluation will only take into account the first character of your answer, so make sure it
1277      contains only one of the identifiers and nothing else (no quotation marks, no spaces, no new
1278      lines, ...).
